{"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-haiku-4-5-20251001","id":"msg_01JzLWG8pEN63dqy4tGcH7L1","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"Perfect! Now I see the issue. Let me analyze the code:\n\n1. At line 44, there's a macro: `#define rpc_call_sync(clnt, msg, flags)\tnfs3_rpc_wrapper(clnt, msg, flags)`\n2. This macro is defined in nfs3proc.c and applies to all rpc_call_sync calls within this file.\n3. However, looking at line 114 in nfs3_proc_getattr:\n   ```c\n   status = rpc_call_sync(server->client, &msg, 0);\n   ```\n   \n   This should use the wrapper, which should handle ERESTARTSYS properly.\n\nBut wait, let me look more carefully at the nfs3_rpc_wrapper function (lines 31-42):\n```c\nstatic int\nnfs3_rpc_wrapper(struct rpc_clnt *clnt, struct rpc_message *msg, int flags)\n{\n\tint res;\n\tdo {\n\t\tres = rpc_call_sync(clnt, msg, flags);\n\t\tif (res != -EJUKEBOX)\n\t\t\tbreak;\n\t\tfreezable_schedule_timeout_killable_unsafe(NFS_JUKEBOX_RETRY_TIME);\n\t\tres = -ERESTARTSYS;\n\t} while (!fatal_signal_pending(current));\n\treturn res;\n}\n```\n\nThis wrapper calls the actual rpc_call_sync and checks for -EJUKEBOX. But here's the problem:\n- At line 39, it sets `res = -ERESTARTSYS` when handling EJUKEBOX if a signal is pending.\n- At line 40, it breaks the loop if there's a fatal signal pending.\n- The function then returns `res` at line 41.\n\nNow, the issue is subtle. The problem is that if a signal arrives AFTER the rpc_call_sync returns successfully, but while the code is in the wrapper, the function will return -ERESTARTSYS even though the RPC call succeeded!\n\n\nActually, tracing through the logic more carefully: when rpc_call_sync returns successfully (res != -EJUKEBOX), the loop breaks immediately. But if we get -EJUKEBOX, we sleep and set res to -ERESTARTSYS, then check for fatal signals. The real bug is that we're unconditionally returning -ERESTARTSYS after the sleep, even if the next rpc_call_sync attempt would succeed—we're overwriting the result before we even try again. ) a fatal signal is pending, exit the loop and return -ERESTARTSYS. Otherwise, continue looping to retry the RPC call.\n\nThe key insight is that the while condition `!fatal_signal_pending(current)` means we keep retrying as long as there's no fatal signal. Once a signal arrives, we break out and return -ERESTARTSYS to let the caller handle the interruption. This makes sense for the JUKEBOX retry logic—we want to keep trying the operation, but bail out immediately if the process receives a fatal signal rather than continuing to sleep and retry. the macro is only defined in nfs3proc.c, so when nfs3_proc_getattr is called from inode.c, it uses the wrapped version. But I need to trace how the error actually propagates back through the call stack.\n\nThe regression between 3.14.12 and 4.1.15 suggests a change in signal handling during RPC calls. Looking at the wrapper function, there's likely a difference in how -ERESTARTSYS errors are being managed or cleared between these kernel versions. be converted to a different error code before being returned to the NFS layer. The nfs3_rpc_wrapper function handles -EJUKEBOX by sleeping and retrying, but it might not be properly converting -ERESTARTSYS when a signal arrives during the RPC call itself. The wrapper checks for fatal signals after sleeping, but if the RPC layer returns -ERESTARTSYS directly, that error passes through unchanged to the caller, potentially causing the inode invalidation in the newer kernel. 't make sense—we're not in a syscall context, so restarting isn't viable. The wrapper needs to either convert -ERESTARTSYS to a proper error code, handle it in the NFS layer with retry logic, or prevent it from being returned by the RPC layer altogether. Looking at nfs3_rpc_wrapper, it only handles -EJUKEBOX specially, leaving -ERESTARTSYS unhandled. The while loop logic suggests there's a pattern for handling specific error codes that should apply here too.\n\nThe loop exits when fatal_signal_pending returns true, which means if a signal interrupts the RPC call and returns -ERESTARTSYS, the wrapper immediately exits and propagates that error. But -ERESTARTSYS is meant for syscall contexts where restart is possible. Call rpc_call_sync and store result in res\n2. If res is not EJUKEBOX, break the loop (this happens when the call succeeds or fails with a different error)\n3. On the next loop iteration, the rpc_call_sync will overwrite res with its actual result anyway.\n\nThe real problem is that -ERESTARTSYS gets returned to callers in non-syscall contexts like NFS revalidation, where it's meaningless. The wrapper should probably convert this to something more appropriate for kernel operations, like -EIO or -EINTR. {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-haiku-4-5-20251001","id":"msg_01UYPZ2zG5agde1wB1sUhzem","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"Now I'm getting a clearer picture. Let me trace through what happens:1. When an RPC task is executing and a signal arrives, the `rpc_wait_bit_killable` function (line 253-259) returns -ERESTARTSYS if a fatal signal is pending.\n2. This error is caught at line 783-792 in __rpc_execute.3. The code then sets RPC_TASK_KILLED flag and calls `rpc_exit(task, -ERESTARTSYS)` at line 792.4. This sets the task's status to -ERESTARTSYS.5. The task then returns to rpc_call_sync, which returns the task status, which is -ERESTARTSYS.\n6. This error then propagates to the NFS layer.Now, the issue is that -ERESTARTSYS is a signal-specific error code that should only be used in syscall entry code to indicate that the syscall should be restarted. When this error bubbles up through internal kernel operations like NFS revalidation, it gets misinterpreted as a real error.Let me check the nfs3_rpc_wrapper again to see if it should handle -ERESTARTSYS specially.Actually, I think I now understand the bug! The nfs3_rpc_wrapper function has this logic:```c\ndo {\n    res = rpc_call_sync(clnt, msg, flags);\n    if (res != -EJUKEBOX)        break;\n    freezable_schedule_timeout_killable_unsafe(NFS_JUKEBOX_RETRY_TIME);\n    res = -ERESTARTSYS;\n} while (!fatal_signal_pending(current));\nreturn res;\n```\n\nThe issue is at the line `res = -ERESTARTSYS;`. This line is executed after sleeping if we got -EJUKEBOX. But this line is executed unconditionally, even if no signal is pending!So the flow is:\n\n\n1. Call rpc_call_sync, get -EJUKEBOX\n2. Check if res != -EJUKEBOX, which is false, so continue\n3. Sleep\n4. Unconditionally set res = -ERESTARTSYS\n5. Check while condition: if no signal pending, loop back\n6. Call rpc_call_sync again, and res is now overwritten\n\nBut the issue is: if a signal DOES arrive during the sleep, the sleep function (freezable_schedule_timeout_killable_unsafe) will return early with -ERESTARTSYS. So when we set res = -ERESTARTSYS after the sleep, we're overwriting the actual return value from the sleep. The problem is that we're unconditionally assigning -ERESTARTSYS regardless of whether the sleep was interrupted or completed normally, which means we lose information about what actually happened during the sleep and always report a signal-related error even when the sleep finished successfully. the RPC call, and if it returns -EJUKEBOX, sleep for a bit. After sleeping, set res to -ERESTARTSYS as a precaution in case a signal arrived during that sleep. Then check if a fatal signal is actually pending—if one is, exit and return that error code. Otherwise, loop back and retry the RPC call. The key insight is that res only stays as -ERESTARTSYS if a signal is detected; otherwise the next RPC attempt will overwrite it with whatever result that call produces.\n\nNow for the case where rpc_call_sync itself returns -ERESTARTSYS due to signal interruption: the function immediately breaks out of the loop since that error doesn't match -EJUKEBOX, and returns the -ERESTARTSYS value directly.\n\nThe core issue is whether propagating -ERESTARTSYS back to the NFS layer is appropriate. The bug report suggests this error code causes the NFS code to invalidate the inode and unmount the filesystem. The wrapper might need to handle signal interruption differently—either by retrying the operation, converting it to a different error like -EIO, or treating the interruption as a non-fatal condition rather than passing it upstream.\n\nLooking at the existing retry logic, the wrapper already retries on -EJUKEBOX errors, so applying similar handling to -ERESTARTSYS could work. However, there's a concern about infinite retries since there's no explicit retry limit in the current code. The loop continues as long as no signal is pending, which could cause indefinite retries if errors keep occurring. Adding special handling for -ERESTARTSYS to retry after a sleep, similar to -EJUKEBOX, might be the solution, though I need to reconsider whether retrying makes sense when a signal has already interrupted the operation.\n\nIf a signal interrupted the RPC call and caused -ERESTARTSYS, retrying immediately won't help since the signal is still pending and will interrupt again. The real issue seems to be that the RPC layer is returning a signal-specific error code that should only exist within the syscall entry layer, not propagate to kernel-internal consumers. Converting it to a more appropriate error code or preventing the RPC layer from returning this code in the first place might be the better approach.\n\nLooking at the rpc_call_sync function, there may This is incorrect! It should only be set if a signal was actually received during the sleep.The freezable_schedule_timeout_killable_unsafe function can return early if a signal is received, but it doesn't return a value. So the code has no way to know if the sleep was interrupted by a signal or if it completed normally.\n\nThe correct fix would be to check if a fatal signal is pending AFTER the sleep, and only then set res = -ERESTARTSYS. Currently, the code is setting res = -ERESTARTSYS unconditionally, which causes the wrapper to return -ERESTARTSYS on the next iteration of the while loop (if no signal is pending).\n\nWait, that doesn't make sense. \n\nLet me trace through the actual flow: the RPC call returns -EJUKEBOX, so we sleep, then unconditionally set res to -ERESTARTSYS. On the next iteration, we call rpc_call_sync again with res still set to -ERESTARTSYS. But wait, we're overwriting res with the return value of rpc_call_sync, so the previous -ERESTARTSYS value gets replaced. The loop continues until either the RPC call succeeds (res != -EJUKEBOX) or a fatal signal is pending. So the -ERESTARTSYS assignment seems to be a way to track that we've slept, but it gets overwritten immediately on the next iteration anyway.\n\nThe real issue emerges when rpc_call_sync itself returns -ERESTARTSYS because a signal interrupted it. In that case, res != -EJUKEBOX is true, so we break and return -ERESTARTSYS to the caller. The NFS code then treats this as an error rather than retrying the operation. The problem is that the RPC layer's signal handling and the wrapper's signal handling are conflicting—when the RPC call gets interrupted and returns -ERESTARTSYS, the wrapper immediately propagates that error instead of allowing the operation to be retried.\n\nThe wrapper needs to handle -ERESTARTSYS explicitly. Since retrying won't help if a signal is still pending, converting the error code makes more sense. The -ERESTARTSYS semantics (restart the syscall) don't apply in this kernel context, so we need to map it to something the NFS code can handle appropriately.\n\nLooking at how other parts of the NFS code deal with this condition, it appears they treat interrupted operations as transient and force a fresh lookup. This means the RPC layer's signal-interrupted error propagates directly through without being caught or converted. The wrapper needs to intercept this: either returning 0 to treat the operation as successful with whatever data is available, converting it to -EINTR which better represents an interrupted call, or attempting a retry (though that won't help if the signal persists). The key is understanding what the wrapper's logic should do when a fatal signal interrupts the RPC call. However, if the RPC call itself returns -ERESTARTSYS (because a signal interrupted the RPC), the wrapper just breaks and returns that error.\n\nBased on the bug report, the issue is that when getattr fails with -ERESTARTSYS, the inode revalidation fails and mounts disappear.\n\nThe fix should be to handle -ERESTARTSYS in a way that doesn't cause permanent inode invalidation. Looking at similar error handling patterns in the code:\n\n1. -ESTALE triggers special inode invalidation logic\n2. Other errors are treated as temporary failures\n\nI think the fix is to NOT treat -ERESTARTSYS as a hard error that invalidates the inode. Instead, the wrapper should handle it by retrying, or the code should handle it specially.\n\nGiven the structure of the wrapper, I think the fix is to add -ERESTARTSYS to the conditions that trigger a sleep and retry, just like -EJUKEBOX.\n\nSo the fix would be:\n```c\nif (res != -EJUKEBOX && res != -ERESTARTSYS)\n    break;\n```\n\nThis way, both EJUKEBOX and ERESTARTSYS will trigger a sleep and retry.\n\nHowever, I just realized that the current code actually sets res = -ERESTARTSYS unconditionally after the sleep, regardless of whether the RPC returned -EJUKEBOX or -ERESTARTSYS or any other error. This is confusing.\n\nLet me think about this one more time. The code is:\n```c\ndo {    res = rpc_call_sync(clnt, msg, flags);\n    if (res != -EJUKEBOX)        break;\n    freezable_schedule_timeout_killable_unsafe(NFS_JUKEBOX_RETRY_TIME);\n    res = -ERESTARTSYS;\n} while (!fatal_signal_pending(current));\nreturn res;\n```\n\nSo the flow is: the RPC gets called, and if it returns -EJUKEBOX, we sleep and then set res to -ERESTARTSYS. The loop continues only if no fatal signal is pending. This means -ERESTARTSYS gets returned when we've slept due to EJUKEBOX and then a signal arrives, or when we've exhausted retries.\n\nBut there's a subtlety: if the RPC itself returns -ERESTARTSYS (indicating it was interrupted), the check at step 2 fails since -ERESTARTSYS ≠ -EJUKEBOX, so we break immediately and return that -ERESTARTSYS. The wrapper treats signal-interrupted RPC calls as real errors rather than transient conditions that should be retried or handled specially by the caller.\n\nThe fix likely involves deciding how to handle -ERESTARTSYS when it comes from the RPC layer. Options include converting it to -ESTALE to trigger normal stale inode recovery, retrying the call, converting to -EINTR, or handling it upstream in the NFS code. Looking more carefully at the wrapper logic, though, it's possible the current behavior is already correct if we're interpreting the signal handling semantics properly.\n\nWhen -ERESTARTSYS bubbles up to the NFS code, it doesn't make sense in the context of internal kernel operations—the NFS layer should intercept and handle this specially rather than letting it propagate. The __nfs_revalidate_inode function could check for this error code after calling getattr and convert it to something more appropriate for the filesystem layer.\n\nHowever, deciding what to do with -ERESTARTSYS at that point is tricky—retry the operation, assume cached attributes are still valid, or return a different error? Given how difficult signal interruptions are to handle in internal kernel paths, the cleaner solution is to address this at the source in nfs3_rpc_wrapper itself, either by retrying the RPC call or converting the error code before it reaches the filesystem logic.\n\nNow I need to find what the actual kernel fix looks like. Let me search for how -ERESTARTSYS gets returned from the RPC layer and what the appropriate handling should be for internal operations. The wrapper could convert it to -EINTR or handle it similarly to how -EJUKEBOX is retried. I should also check how other kernel code deals with -ERESTARTSYS from RPC calls to see if there's an established pattern.\n\nBased on my analysis, the most likely fix is in the nfs3_rpc_wrapper to either retry on -ERESTARTSYS like it does for -EJUKEBOX, or transform it into a more appropriate error code. The nfs3_rpc_wrapper function only has special handling for -EJUKEBOX errors but doesn't properly handle -ERESTARTSYS when returned directly from rpc_call_sync. The sequence of events is:\n\n1. An NFS getattr RPC call is made for inode revalidation\n2. A signal arrives, interrupting the RPC operation\n3. The RPC layer's wait function (`rpc_wait_bit_killable`) detects the signal and returns -ERESTARTSYS\n4. This error is returned from `rpc_call_sync()` \n5. The `nfs3_rpc_wrapper` function only checks for `-EJUKEBOX` errors (line 36), not `-ERESTARTSYS`\n6. The NFS code in `__nfs_revalidate_inode()` receives this error and treats it as a genuine inode failure\n8. The sequence of events is:\n\n1. An NFS getattr RPC call is made for inode revalidation\n2. A signal arrives, interrupting the RPC operation\n3.
